Commented on post by Sadovnik Socratus in Quantum PhysicsI don't think so. A rotation is done by a matrix containing sin and cos functions with the rotation angle as parameter. A Lorentz transformation has sinh and cosh instead with rapidity as parameter. It is already as trivial as a rotation in space. Sin, cos, cosh and sinh are related via exp function and complex number algebra. Concerning a) how do you want to define speed when you start with infinitely slow time? In earlier formulations of SRT time was imaginary. With ART this concept was abandoned and the metric tensor g was introduced. Commented on post by Dr. Brian Cameron in Quantum Physics+Randall Lee Reetz So please define "honesty". I think it has something to do with "truth". Perhaps the best would be to first define "truth" first? Then I'd like to know what "normal" is and who may define it. Sorry, I already gave answers to these questions above and will not repeat them here. ‚Äî I was talking with a friend of mine who, like me, has a particular fascination with Physics and, in particular, Quantum Mechanics and a question arose for me: can two Bose-Einstein condensates be placed into super-position (i.e., be in a state of quantum entanglement)? This is, of course, an empirical question that, as far as I know, has not as yet been answered and may not be answerable in the near future. However, either answer carries with it interesting implications. If you consider that quantum entanglement appears to be indifferent to the phase-states of the macro-composite of which the particles in super-position might be located, then there appears to be no particular grounds for thinking that this would not hold good for all phases of matter, including the Bose-Einstein condensate. However, that particular phase of matter is one in which some relatively large number of atoms function "as if" they were one atom -- a mesoscale property of matter too large to be wholly understood by QM but too small to be entirely subject to SR either. On the other hand, ruling out the Bose-Einstein condensate as a possible item within a quantumly entangled state appears arbitrary. Why would this or any phase of matter obviate against particles coming into superposition unless, of course, the Bose-Einstein condensate only appears to be another form of matter? Since I'm not a physicist, let's see what some of you think. Pax!

Commented on post by Dr. Brian Cameron in Quantum Physics+Randall Lee Reetz Motives of human beings haven't changed in the past 10000 years. So i think no matter what they are doing, in the average they are driven by the same things as always. And I believe mostly it is maximation of joy and minimation of pain. And this is true also for most phycisists, whatever gives them pain and joy when they are doing their business. And to cite Schopenhauer: "Der Wille ist immer grundlos." = There is never a final reason why someone is doing something. Every child can find this out by asking any adult a chain of why-questions "Why are you doing this?" "Because of blah" "Why?" ... I'm not an experimental phycisist. Yes, I have a diploma in physics. What drives me personally are philosophical questions that everyone has: Who am I? What kind of world is this? Why do I fear death? ... Believing to have some answers, as a phycisist I know for sure that you can discuss them with colleagues only if you can proove that you understand all their stuff. Otherwise they won't take you seriously (in your role as philosopher). So I believe my answers can remove pain and fear from your human live, but I must proove that they fit into scientific observations (observations, not theories). You have eyes, ears, a tongue and so on, which give you a few perceptions of the outside world. Science just gives you more and better senses. The better, the more expensive the senses are. ‚Äî I was talking with a friend of mine who, like me, has a particular fascination with Physics and, in particular, Quantum Mechanics and a question arose for me: can two Bose-Einstein condensates be placed into super-position (i.e., be in a state of quantum entanglement)? If you consider that quantum entanglement appears to be indifferent to the phase-states of the macro-composite of which the particles in super-position might be located, then there appears to be no particular grounds for thinking that this would not hold good for all phases of matter, including the Bose-Einstein condensate. However, that particular phase of matter is one in which some relatively large number of atoms function "as if" they were one atom -- a mesoscale property of matter too large to be wholly understood by QM but too small to be entirely subject to SR either. On the other hand, ruling out the Bose-Einstein condensate as a possible item within a quantumly entangled state appears arbitrary. Why would this or any phase of matter obviate against particles coming into superposition unless, of course, the Bose-Einstein condensate only appears to be another form of matter? Since I'm not a physicist, let's see what some of you think. Pax!

Shared to the community Quantum Physics - √ñffentlich
Commented on post by Dr. Brian Cameron in Quantum PhysicsAny state vectors can be combined in a superposition, also 2 state vectors that stand for Bose-Einstein condensates. This is due to the mathematical structure of quantum mechanics (its linearity). On the other hand a superposition is not absolute. Any state vector has an infinite number of representations, this depends on the choose of the basis (basis ambiguity). While in many bases any state vector looks like a superposition, in others it doesn't. But what you describe are not 2 different states of 1 system. You mean 2 systems, each living in a subspace of the total Hilbert space. You can't build a superposition of vectors in different subspaces. You only can build superpositions of vectors, that live in the same space. In contrast to superposition, entanglement is something between subspaces. Any subspaces of the total Hilbert space can be entangled, no matter what their states represent (e.g. Bose-Einstein condensates). But to be exact: as soon as they are entangled, they don't have state vectors any more! Only the whole space can be described by a state vector. Hope this helps. ‚Äî I was talking with a friend of mine who, like me, has a particular fascination with Physics and, in particular, Quantum Mechanics and a question arose for me: can two Bose-Einstein condensates be placed into super-position (i.e., be in a state of quantum entanglement)? Commented on post by Fabiana Espistto in Quantum PhysicsAnd the most funny is: every state vector is pure but can deliver both entangled and pure subsystems at the same time, because entanglement is not a property of a state vector. Concerning entanglement all Hilbert space vectors are equivalent: they are pure. The entanglement measure (e.g. von Neumann entropy) depends on state vector + thought division of the space into 2 parts, the latter one leading to a unique Schmidt decomposition for the state vector - and to an entanglement between the parts possibly > 0. ‚Äî Entanglement is of central importance for new #quantum technologies. A German-Austrian research team is now presenting the largest entangled quantum register of individually controllable systems to date, consisting of a total of 20 quantum bits. The physicists in #Innsbruck , Vienna and Ulm are pushing experimental and theoretical methods to the limits of what is currently possible. Some of the new quantum technologies ranging from extremely precise sensors to universal quantum computers require a large number of quantum bits in order to exploit the advantages of quantum physics. Physicists all over the world are therefore working on implementing entangled systems with more and more quantum bits. The record is currently held by Rainer Blatt's research group at the Institute of Experimental Physics at the University of Innsbruck. In 2011, the physicists entangled 14 individually addressable quantum bits for the first time and thus realized the largest completely entangled quantum register. Now, a research team led by Ben Lanyon and Rainer Blatt at the Institute of Quantum Optics and Quantum Information (IQOQI) of the Austrian Academy of Sciences, together with theorists from the University of Ulm and the Institute of Quantum Optics and Quantum Information in Vienna, has now realized controlled multi-particle entanglement in a system of 20 quantum bits. The researchers were able to detect genuine multi-particle entanglement between all neighbouring groups of three, four and five quantum bits. => article written BY University of Innsbruck office, all credicts, read more at: https://www.uibk.ac.at/newsroom/quantum-physicists-achieve-entanglement-record.html.en
